890 <section id="_1">

891

892 <h2>

893General Information About Acute Myeloid Leukemia (AML)</h2>

894

895 <div id="_section_1" class="pdq-sections">

896<nav class="in-this-section" role="navigation">

897<h6>

898In This Section</h6>

899<ul>

900<li>

901<a href="#_359_toc">

902Incidence and Mortality</a>

903</li>

904<li>

905<a href="#_449_toc">

906Anatomy</a>

907</li>

908<li>

909<a href="#_450_toc">

910Clinical Presentation</a>

911</li>

912<li>

913<a href="#_451_toc">

914Diagnostic Evaluation</a>

915</li>

916<li>

917<a href="#_393_toc">

918Prognosis and Prognostic Factors</a>

919</li>

920<li>

921<a href="#_459_toc">

922Long-term Effects of Cancer Treatment</a>

923</li>

924</ul>

925</nav>

926<div class="pdq-hp-patient-toggle">

927<a href="/types/leukemia/patient/adult-aml-treatment-pdq">

928Go to Patient Version</a>

929</div>

930<p id="_448" tabindex="-1">

931AML is also called

932acute myelogenous leukemia and acute nonlymphocytic leukemia.</p>

933<section id="_359">

934<h3 id="_359_toc">

935Incidence and Mortality</h3>

936<p id="_258" tabindex="-1">

937Estimated new cases and deaths from AML in the United States in 2023:[<a href="#cit/section_1.1">

9381</a>

939]</p>

940<div class="pdq-content-list">

941<ul id="_259">

942<li>

943New cases: 20,380.</li>

944<li>

945Deaths: 11,310.</li>

946</ul>

947</div>

948<p id="_577" tabindex="-1">

949Based on Surveillance, Epidemiology, and End Results (SEER) Program data from 2012 to 2018, 30.5% of patients with AML were alive 5 years after diagnosis.[<a href="#cit/section_1.2">

9502</a>

951]</p>

952</section>

953<section id="_449">

954<h3 id="_449_toc">

955Anatomy</h3>

956<figure id="figure_568" class="image-center">

957<a href="https://nci-media.cancer.gov/pdq/media/images/526538.jpg" target="_blank" class="article-image-enlarge">

958Enlarge</a>

959<img id="_568" alt="Blood cell development; drawing shows the steps a blood stem cell goes through to become a red blood cell, platelet, or white blood cell. A myeloid stem cell becomes a red blood cell, a platelet, or a myeloblast, which then becomes a granulocyte (the types of granulocytes are eosinophils, basophils, and neutrophils). A lymphoid stem cell becomes a lymphoblast and then becomes a B-lymphocyte, T-lymphocyte, or natural killer cell." title="Blood cell development; drawing shows the steps a blood stem cell goes through to become a red blood cell, platelet, or white blood cell. A myeloid stem cell becomes a red blood cell, a platelet, or a myeloblast, which then becomes a granulocyte (the types of granulocytes are eosinophils, basophils, and neutrophils). A lymphoid stem cell becomes a lymphoblast and then becomes a B-lymphocyte, T-lymphocyte, or natural killer cell." src="https://nci-media.cancer.gov/pdq/media/images/526538-750.jpg">

960<figcaption class="caption-container">

961Blood cell development. A blood stem cell goes through several steps to become a red blood cell, platelet, or white blood cell. </figcaption>

962</figure>

963<p id="_578" tabindex="-1">

964AML is a heterogenous group of blood cancers that arise as a result of clonal expansion of myeloid hematopoietic precursors in the bone marrow. Not only are circulating leukemia cells (also called blasts) seen in the peripheral blood, but granulocytopenia, anemia, and thrombocytopenia are also common as proliferating leukemia cells interfere with normal hematopoiesis.[<a href="#cit/section_1.3">

9653</a>

966] </p>

967</section>

968<section id="_450">

969<h3 id="_450_toc">

970Clinical Presentation</h3>

971<p id="_579" tabindex="-1">

972The diagnosis of AML is uncommon before age 45 years; the median age at diagnosis is 68 years.[<a href="#cit/section_1.2">

9732</a>

974] Patients may present with symptoms that include the following:</p>

975<div class="pdq-content-list">

976<ul id="_580">

977<li>

978Weakness.</li>

979<li>

980Fever.</li>

981<li>

982Infection.</li>

983<li>

984Pallor. </li>

985<li>

986Bleeding. </li>

987</ul>

988</div>

989<p id="_581" tabindex="-1">

990The hampered production of normal blood cells due to leukemic infiltration of the bone marrow can also cause other symptoms and complications. Less commonly, patients have signs or symptoms related to the collection of leukemia cells in certain anatomic locations, such as central nervous system (CNS) or testicular involvement, or the presence of a myeloid sarcoma (also called chloroma). The symptoms of acute leukemia often arise over a 4- to 6-week period before diagnosis.[<a href="#cit/section_1.3">

9913</a>

992]</p>

993</section>

994<section id="_451">

995<h3 id="_451_toc">

996Diagnostic Evaluation</h3>

997<p id="_8" tabindex="-1">

998The differentiation of AML from other forms of leukemia, in particular chronic myelogenous leukemia and acute lymphocytic leukemia, has vital

999therapeutic implications. The primary diagnostic tool in this determination is flow cytometry to evaluate surface antigens on the leukemia cells. Simple morphology is not adequate in determining lineage and, at a minimum, special histochemical stains are needed. While a diagnosis can be made by evaluating peripheral blood, a bone marrow biopsy is used to evaluate morphology and cell surface markers, as well as provide material for cytogenetic and molecular analysis. A peripheral blood or bone marrow blast count of 20% or greater is required to make the diagnosis, except for cases with certain chromosomal abnormalities (i.e., t(15;17), t(8;21), inv(16), or t(16;16)).[<a href="#cit/section_1.4">

10004</a>

1001]</p>

1002</section>

1003<section id="_393">

1004<h3 id="_393_toc">

1005Prognosis and Prognostic Factors</h3>

1006<p id="_2" tabindex="-1">

1007Advances in the treatment of AML have resulted in substantially improved

1008complete remission (CR) rates.[<a href="#cit/section_1.2">

10092</a>

1010] Treatment should be sufficiently aggressive to

1011achieve CR because partial remission offers no substantial

1012survival benefit. Approximately 60% to 70% of adults with AML can be expected

1013to attain CR status after appropriate induction therapy.

1014More than 25% of adults with AML (about 45% of those who attain CR) can be expected to survive 3 or more years and may be cured.</p>

1015<p id="_452" tabindex="-1">

1016Approximately half of patients with AML will harbor chromosomal abnormalities; therefore, conventional cytogenetic analysis remains mandatory in the evaluation of suspected AML.[<a href="#cit/section_1.5">

10175</a>

1018,<a href="#cit/section_1.6">

10196</a>

1020] With the routine use of molecular diagnostics, the identification of recurrent somatic mutations in <em class="gene-name">

1021NPM1</em>

1022, <em class="gene-name">

1023FLT3</em>

1024, <em class="gene-name">

1025CEPBA</em>

1026, and <em class="gene-name">

1027RUNX1</em>

1028, among other genes, has become a routine part of determining prognosis. Cytogenetic and molecular analyses provide the strongest prognostic information

1029available, predicting outcome of both remission induction and postremission

1030therapy.[<a href="#cit/section_1.7">

10317</a>

1032] Cytogenic and molecular information has been combined to form distinct prognostic groups.</p>

1033<p id="_453" tabindex="-1">

1034Additional adverse prognostic factors for AML include the following:</p>

1035<div class="pdq-content-list">

1036<ul id="_454">

1037<li>

1038Age at diagnosis. Remission rates in adult AML are inversely related to age, with an expected

1039remission rate of more than 65% for those younger than 60 years.

1040Data suggest that once attained, duration of remission may be shorter in older

1041patients. Increased morbidity and mortality during induction appear to be

1042directly related to age.</li>

1043<li>

1044 CNS involvement with leukemia.</li>

1045<li>

1046Systemic infection at diagnosis.</li>

1047<li>

1048Elevated white blood cell count (&gt;100,000/mm<span class="sup">

10493</span>

1050) at diagnosis.</li>

1051<li>

1052<a href="/types/leukemia/hp/adult-aml-treatment-pdq#_139">

1053Therapy-related myeloid neoplasms</a>

1054, resulting from alkylating agents and radiation therapy.</li>

1055<li>

1056History of myelodysplastic syndrome or another antecedent hematological disorder.</li>

1057</ul>

1058</div>

1059</section>

1060<section id="_459">

1061<h3 id="_459_toc">

1062Long-term Effects of Cancer Treatment</h3>

1063<p id="_582" tabindex="-1">

1064The risk of developing any long-term effects depends on the type and dose of treatment that was used and the age at which the patient underwent treatment.</p>

1065<p id="_105" tabindex="-1">

1066A study of 30 patients who had AML that was in remission for at least 10 years demonstrated a 13% incidence of secondary malignancies.[<a href="#cit/section_1.8">

10678</a>

1068] Of 31 female long-term survivors of AML or acute lymphoblastic leukemia (ALL) diagnosed before age 40 years, 26 resumed normal menstruation after completion of therapy. Among 36 live offspring of survivors, two congenital problems occurred.[<a href="#cit/section_1.8">

10698</a>

1070]</p>

1071<p id="_583" tabindex="-1">

1072Most patients with AML who undergo intensive therapy are treated with an anthracycline. Anthracyclines have been associated with increased risk of congestive heart failure (CHF).[<a href="#cit/section_1.9">

10739</a>

1074] Anthracycline cardiotoxicity is dose-dependent. In one study, doxorubicin-related CHF was 5% at a lifetime cumulative dose of 400 mg/m<span class="sup">

10752</span>

1076, rising to 26% at a cumulative dose of 550 mg/m<span class="sup">

10772</span>

1078.[<a href="#cit/section_1.10">

107910</a>

1080] In many cases, heart failure can manifest as a late effect.[<a href="#cit/section_1.11">

108111</a>

1082] In an analysis of children who underwent treatment for acute leukemia, the cumulative incidence of CHF at 10 years was 1.7% in ALL and 7.5% in AML.[<a href="#cit/section_1.12">

108312</a>

1084]</p>

1085<p id="_584" tabindex="-1">

1086Patients who undergo allogeneic hematopoietic stem cell transplantation can experience a large number of long-term or late side effects of treatment as a result of high-dose chemotherapy and/or radiation, and as an effect of chronic graft-versus-host disease and immunosuppression. These side effects may include chronic fatigue, thyroid and gonadal dysfunction, infertility, chronic infection, accelerated coronary heart disease, osteopenia, cataracts, iron overload, adverse psychological outcomes, and second cancers.[<a href="#cit/section_1.13">

108713</a>

1088-<a href="#cit/section_1.15">

108915</a>

1090] </p>

1091<p id="_656" tabindex="-1">

1092In the Bone Marrow Transplant Survivor Study, hematopoietic cell transplantation survivors had accelerated aging and were 8.4 times more likely to be frail than their siblings (95% confidence interval [CI], 2.0&#8722;34.5; <em>

1093P</em>

1094&#8201;=&#8201;.003). In a multivariable analysis, frailty was associated with a 2.76-fold increase in the risk of death, compared with a nonfrail state (95% CI, 1.7&#8722;4.4; <em>

1095P</em>

1096&#8201;&lt;&#8201;.001).[<a href="#cit/section_1.16">

109716</a>

1098] </p>

1099</section>

1266<section id="_460">

1267<h3 id="_460_toc">

1268World Health Organization (WHO) Classification</h3>

1269<p id="_4" tabindex="-1">

1270The classification of acute myeloid leukemia (AML) has been revised by a group of pathologists and clinicians under the auspices of the WHO.[<a href="#cit/section_2.1">

12711</a>

1272] While elements of the French-American-British (FAB) classification have been retained (i.e., morphology, immunophenotype, cytogenetics, and clinical features),[<a href="#cit/section_2.2">

12732</a>

1274,<a href="#cit/section_2.3">

12753</a>

1276] the WHO classification incorporates and interrelates morphology, cytogenetics, molecular genetics, and immunologic markers, which construct a classification that is universally applicable and has prognostic and therapeutic relevance.[<a href="#cit/section_2.1">

12771</a>

1278,<a href="#cit/section_2.3">

12793</a>

1280,<a href="#cit/section_2.4">

12814</a>

1282] Each criterion has prognostic and treatment implications but, for practical purposes, initial antileukemic

1283therapy is similar for all subtypes.</p>

1284<p id="_461" tabindex="-1">

1285In 2001, the WHO proposed a new classification system that incorporated diagnostic cytogenetic information and that more reliably correlated with outcome. This classification system also decreased the bone marrow percentage of leukemic blast requirement for the diagnosis of AML from 30% to 20%. An additional clarification was made so patients with recurrent cytogenetic abnormalities did not need to meet the minimum blast requirement to be considered as having an AML diagnosis.[<a href="#cit/section_2.5">

12865</a>

1287-<a href="#cit/section_2.7">

12887</a>

1289]</p>

1290<p id="_462" tabindex="-1">

1291In 2008, the WHO expanded the number of cytogenetic abnormalities linked to AML classification and, for the first time, included specific gene mutations (<em class="gene-name">

1292CEBPA</em>

1293 and <em class="gene-name">

1294NPM</em>

1295) in its classification system.[<a href="#cit/section_2.5">

12965</a>

1297,<a href="#cit/section_2.8">

12988</a>

1299] With the addition of these gene mutations, FAB subclassification no longer provided prognostic information for patients with a diagnosis of AML, not otherwise specified (NOS).[<a href="#cit/section_2.9">

13009</a>

1301] </p>

1302<p id="_585" tabindex="-1">

1303In 2016, the WHO classification underwent revisions to incorporate the expanding knowledge of leukemia biomarkers that are significantly important to the diagnosis, prognosis, and treatment of leukemia.[<a href="#cit/section_2.10">

130410</a>

1305] With emerging technologies aimed at genetic, epigenetic, proteomic, and immunophenotypic classification, AML classification will continue to evolve and provide informative prognostic and biologic guidelines to clinicians and researchers.</p>

1306<section id="_463">

1443<section id="_119">

1444<h3 id="_119_toc">

1445AML With Recurrent Genetic Abnormalities</h3>

1446<p id="_120" tabindex="-1">

1447AML with well-defined genetic abnormalities is characterized by recurrent genetic abnormalities.[<a href="#cit/section_2.10">

144810</a>

1449] The reciprocal translocations t(8;21), inv(16) or t(16;16), t(15;17), and translocations involving the 11q23 breakpoint are the most commonly identified chromosomal abnormalities. These structural chromosome rearrangements result in the formation of fusion genes that encode chimeric proteins that may contribute to the initiation or progression of leukemogenesis. Many of these translocations are detected by either reverse transcriptase&#8211;polymerase chain reaction (RT&#8211;PCR) or fluorescence <em>

1450in situ</em>

1451 hybridization (FISH), which has a higher sensitivity than metaphase cytogenetics. Other recurring cytogenetic abnormalities are less common.</p>

1452<p id="_586" tabindex="-1">

1453Molecular diagnostic platforms such as next-generation sequencing along with RT-PCR are used to identify recurrent molecular abnormalities in AML, helping to further refine diagnostic categories in the 2016 WHO classification system.[<a href="#cit/section_2.10">

145410</a>

1455]</p>

1456<p id="_121" tabindex="-1">

1457<strong>

1458AML with t(8;21)(q22;q22), <em class="gene-name">

1459RUNX1</em>

1460-<em class="gene-name">

1461RUNX1T1</em>

1462</strong>

1463</p>

1464<p id="_122" tabindex="-1">

1465The translocation t(8;21)(q22;q22) is one of the most common chromosomal aberrations in AML and accounts for 5% to 12% of cases.[<a href="#cit/section_2.11">

146611</a>

1467] Myeloid sarcomas (chloromas) may be present and may be associated with a bone marrow blast percentage of less than 20%. </p>

1468<p id="_248" tabindex="-1">

1469Common morphologic features include the following: </p>

1470<div class="pdq-content-list">

1471<ul id="_249">

1472<li>

1473Large blasts with abundant basophilic cytoplasm, often containing numerous azurophilic granules. </li>

1474<li>

1475A few blasts in some cases show very large granules (pseudo Chediak-Higashi granules).</li>

1476<li>

1477Auer rods,

1478which may be detected in mature neutrophils. </li>

1479<li>

1480Smaller blasts, predominantly in the peripheral blood.</li>

1481<li>

1482Promyelocytes, myelocytes, and mature neutrophils with variable dysplasia in the bone marrow.</li>

1483<li>

1484Abnormal nuclear segmentation (pseudo Pelger-Hu&#235;t nuclei) and/or cytoplasmic staining abnormalities.</li>

1485<li>

1486Increased eosinophil precursors.</li>

1487<li>

1488Reduced or absent monocytes. </li>

1489<li>

1490Normal erythroblasts and megakaryocytes.</li>

1491</ul>

1492</div>

1493<p id="_250" tabindex="-1">

1494Rarely, AML with this translocation presents with a bone marrow blast percentage of less than 20%.[<a href="#cit/section_2.5">

14955</a>

1496] Along with inv(16)(p13;q22) or t(16;16)(p13;q22), AML with t(8;21) makes up a category known as core binding factor AML. This category of AML is associated with long-term survival when treated with high-dose cytarabine.[<a href="#cit/section_2.12">

149712</a>

1498-<a href="#cit/section_2.15">

149915</a>

1500]</p>

1501<p id="_123" tabindex="-1">

1502The translocation t(8;21)(q22;q22) involves the <em class="gene-name">

1503RUNX1</em>

1504 gene, which encodes CBF-alpha, and the <em class="gene-name">

1505RUNX1T1</em>

1506 (8;21) gene.[<a href="#cit/section_2.5">

15075</a>

1508,<a href="#cit/section_2.16">

150916</a>

1510] The <em class="gene-name">

1511RUNX1/RUNX1T1</em>

1512 fusion transcript is consistently detected in patients with t(8;21) AML. This translocation is usually associated with a good response to chemotherapy and a high complete remission (CR) rate with long-term survival when treated with high-dose cytarabine in the postremission phase, as demonstrated in the Cancer and Leukemia Group B (CLB-9022 and CLB-8525) trials.[<a href="#cit/section_2.12">

151312</a>

1514-<a href="#cit/section_2.15">

151515</a>

1516] Additional chromosome abnormalities are common, for example, loss of a sex chromosome and del(9)(q22). Leukocytosis (i.e., white blood count &gt;25 x 10<span class="sup">

15179</span>

1518/L) is associated with an inferior outcome,[<a href="#cit/section_2.17">

151917</a>

1520] as is the presence of a <em class="gene-name">

1521KIT</em>

1522 mutation.[<a href="#cit/section_2.18">

152318</a>

1524] </p>

1525<p id="_124" tabindex="-1">

1526<strong>

1527AML with inv(16)(p13.1;q22) or t(16;16)(p13.1;q22), <em class="gene-name">

1528CBFB-MYH11</em>

1529</strong>

1530</p>

1531<p id="_125" tabindex="-1">

1532The inv(16)(p13;q22) abnormality or t(16;16)(p13;q22) translocation is found in approximately 10% to 12% of all cases of AML, predominantly in younger patients.[<a href="#cit/section_2.5">

15335</a>

1534,<a href="#cit/section_2.19">

153519</a>

1536] Myeloid sarcomas may be present at initial diagnosis or at relapse. </p>

1537<p id="_192" tabindex="-1">

1538Common morphologic features include the following: </p>

1539<div class="pdq-content-list">

1540<ul id="_193">

1541<li>

1542Monocytic and granulocytic differentiation.</li>

1543<li>

1544A characteristically abnormal eosinophil component with immature purple-violet eosinophil granules that may obscure cell morphology if present in great numbers.</li>

1545<li>

1546Auer rods in myeloblasts.</li>

1547<li>

1548Decreased neutrophils in bone marrow.</li>

1549</ul>

1550</div>

1551<p id="_194" tabindex="-1">

1552As is found in rare cases of AML with t(8;21), the bone marrow blast percentage in this AML is occasionally less than 20%.</p>

1553<p id="_126" tabindex="-1">

1554Both inv(16)(p13;q22) and t(16;16)(p13;q22) result in the fusion of the <em class="gene-name">

1555CBFB</em>

1556 gene at 16q22 to the smooth muscle <em class="gene-name">

1557MYH11</em>

1558 gene at 16p13, thereby forming the fusion gene <em class="gene-name">

1559CBFB/MYH11</em>

1560.[<a href="#cit/section_2.11">

156111</a>

1562] The use of FISH and RT&#8211;PCR methods is sometimes necessary to document this fusion gene because its presence is not always documented by traditional cytogenetics banding techniques.[<a href="#cit/section_2.20">

156320</a>

1564] Similar to AML with t(8;21), patients with the <em class="gene-name">

1565CBFB/MYH11</em>

1566 fusion gene achieve higher CR rates and long-term survival when treated with high-dose cytarabine in the postremission setting.[<a href="#cit/section_2.12">

156712</a>

1568,<a href="#cit/section_2.13">

156913</a>

1570,<a href="#cit/section_2.15">

157115</a>

1572] Unlike AML with t(8;21), the prognostic relevance of <em class="gene-name">

1573KIT</em>

1574 mutations is unclear.[<a href="#cit/section_2.21">

157521</a>

1576]</p>

1577<p id="_127" tabindex="-1">

1578<strong>

1579APL with <em class="gene-name">

1580PML-RARA</em>

1581</strong>

1582</p>

1583<p id="_128" tabindex="-1">

1584APL is defined by the presence of the PML-RARA fusion protein, typically a result of t(15;17)(q22;q12), but can be cryptic or result from complex cytogenetic rearrangements other than t(15;17)(q22;q12). It is also an AML in which promyelocytes are the dominant leukemic cell type. APL exists as two subtypes, hypergranular or typical APL and microgranular or hypogranular APL. APL comprises 5% to 8% of cases of AML and occurs predominately in adults in midlife.[<a href="#cit/section_2.5">

15855</a>

1586] Both typical and microgranular APL are commonly associated with disseminated intravascular coagulation (DIC).[<a href="#cit/section_2.22">

158722</a>

1588,<a href="#cit/section_2.23">

158923</a>

1590] In microgranular APL, unlike typical APL, the leukocyte count can be very high with a rapid doubling time.[<a href="#cit/section_2.5">

15915</a>

1592] </p>

1593<p id="_195" tabindex="-1">

1594Common morphologic features of typical APL include the following:</p>

1595<div class="pdq-content-list">

1596<ul id="_196">

1597<li>

1598Kidney-shaped or bilobed nuclei.</li>

1599<li>

1600Cytoplasm densely packed with large granules (bright pink, red, or purple in Romanowsky stains).</li>

1601<li>

1602Bundles of Auer rods within the cytoplasm (faggot cells).</li>

1603<li>

1604Larger Auer rods than in other types of AML.</li>

1605<li>

1606Strongly positive myeloperoxidase (MPO) reaction in all leukemic promyelocytes.</li>

1607<li>

1608Only occasional leukemic promyelocytes in the

1609blood.</li>

1610</ul>

1611</div>

1612<p id="_197" tabindex="-1">

1613Common morphologic features of microgranular APL include the following:</p>

1614<div class="pdq-content-list">

1615<ul id="_198">

1616<li>

1617Bilobed nuclear shape.</li>

1618<li>

1619Apparent scarce or absent granules (submicroscopic azurophilic granules).</li>

1620<li>

1621Small number of abnormal promyelocytes with visible granules and/or bundles of Auer rods (faggot cells).</li>

1622<li>

1623High leukocyte count in the peripheral blood.</li>

1624<li>

1625Strongly positive MPO reaction in all leukemic promyelocytes.</li>

1626</ul>

1627</div>

1628<p id="_129" tabindex="-1">

1629In APL, the <em class="gene-name">

1630RARA</em>

1631 gene on 17q12 fuses with a nuclear regulatory factor on 15q22 (<em class="gene-name">

1632PML</em>

1633 gene) resulting in a <em class="gene-name">

1634PML/RARA</em>

1635 gene fusion transcript.[<a href="#cit/section_2.24">

163624</a>

1637-<a href="#cit/section_2.26">

163826</a>

1639] Rare cases of cryptic or masked t(15;17) lack typical cytogenetic findings and involve complex variant translocations or submicroscopic insertion of the <em class="gene-name">

1640RARA</em>

1641 gene into the <em class="gene-name">

1642PML</em>

1643 gene, leading to the expression of the <em class="gene-name">

1644PML</em>

1645/<em class="gene-name">

1646RARA</em>

1647 fusion transcript.[<a href="#cit/section_2.5">

16485</a>

1649] FISH and/or RT&#8211;PCR methods may be required to unmask these cryptic genetic rearrangements.[<a href="#cit/section_2.27">

165027</a>

1651,<a href="#cit/section_2.28">

165228</a>

1653] In approximately 1% of the patients with APL, variant chromosomal aberrations may be found in which the <em class="gene-name">

1654RARA</em>

1655 gene is fused with other genes.[<a href="#cit/section_2.29">

165629</a>

1657] Variant translocations involving the <em class="gene-name">

1658RARA</em>

1659 gene include t(11;17)(q23;q21), t(5;17)(q32;q12), and t(11;17)(q13;q21).[<a href="#cit/section_2.5">

16605</a>

1661]</p>

1662<p id="_130" tabindex="-1">

1663APL has a specific sensitivity to treatment with all-<em>

1664trans</em>

1665 retinoic acid (ATRA, tretinoin), which acts as a differentiating agent.[<a href="#cit/section_2.30">

166630</a>

1667-<a href="#cit/section_2.32">

166832</a>

1669] High CR rates and long-term disease-free survival in APL may be obtained by combining ATRA treatment with chemotherapy,[<a href="#cit/section_2.33">

167033</a>

1671] or in a chemotherapy-free regimen with arsenic trioxide.[<a href="#cit/section_2.34">

167234</a>

1673] </p>

1674<p id="_131" tabindex="-1">

1675<strong>

1676AML with t(9;11)(p21.3;q23.3), <em class="gene-name">

1677MLLT3</em>

1678-<em class="gene-name">

1679KMT2A</em>

1680</strong>

1681</p>

1682<p id="_132" tabindex="-1">

1683AML with 11q23 abnormalities comprises 5% to 6% of cases of AML and is typically associated with monocytic features. This type of AML is more common in children. Two clinical subgroups who have a high frequency of AML with 11q23 abnormalities are infants with AML and patients with therapy-related AML, usually occurring after treatment with DNA topoisomerase inhibitors. Patients may present with DIC and extramedullary monocytic sarcomas and/or tissue infiltration (gingiva, skin).[<a href="#cit/section_2.5">

16845</a>

1685] </p>

1686<p id="_199" tabindex="-1">

1687Common morphologic features include the following: </p>

1688<div class="pdq-content-list">

1689<ul id="_200">

1690<li>

1691Monoblasts and promonocytes predominate in the bone marrow.</li>

1692<li>

1693Monoblasts and promonocytes with strong, positive nonspecific-esterase reactions.</li>

1694</ul>

1695</div>

1696<p id="_133" tabindex="-1">

1697The <em class="gene-name">

1698MLLT3</em>

1699 gene on 11q23, an epigenetic regulator, is involved in translocations with approximately 135 different rearrangements having been identified so far.[<a href="#cit/section_2.35">

170035</a>

1701] Genes other than <em class="gene-name">

1702MLLT3</em>

1703 may be involved in 11q23 abnormalities.[<a href="#cit/section_2.36">

170436</a>

1705] FISH may be required to detect genetic abnormalities involving <em class="gene-name">

1706MLL</em>

1707.[<a href="#cit/section_2.36">

170836</a>

1709-<a href="#cit/section_2.38">

171038</a>

1711] In general, risk categories and prognoses for individual 11q23 translocations are difficult to determine because of the lack of studies involving significant numbers of patients; however, patients with t(11;19)(q23;p13.1) have been reported to have poor outcomes.[<a href="#cit/section_2.13">

171213</a>

1713] </p>

1714<section id="_468">

1715<h4 id="_468_toc">

1716AML with t(6;9)(p23;q34.1), <em class="gene-name">

1717DEK-NUP214</em>

1718</h4>

1719<p id="_587" tabindex="-1">

1720The t(6;9) translocation leads to the formation of a leukemia-associated fusion protein DEK-NUP214 and accounts for approximately 1% of AML cases.[<a href="#cit/section_2.39">

172139</a>

1722-<a href="#cit/section_2.41">

172341</a>

1724] NUP214 is a component of the nuclear pore complex. This subgroup of AML has been associated with a poor prognosis.[<a href="#cit/section_2.39">

172539</a>

1726,<a href="#cit/section_2.42">

172742</a>

1728,<a href="#cit/section_2.43">

172943</a>

1730] </p>

1731</section>

1732<section id="_469">

1733<h4 id="_469_toc">

1734AML with inv(3)(q21.3;q26.2) or t(3;3)(q21.3;q26.2), <em class="gene-name">

1735GATA2, MECOM</em>

1736</h4>

1737<p id="_588" tabindex="-1">

1738The inv(3) abnormality or t(3;3) translocation occur infrequently and account for approximately 1% of all AML cases.[<a href="#cit/section_2.41">

173941</a>

1740] <em class="gene-name">

1741MECOM</em>

1742 at chromosome 3q26 codes for two proteins, EVI1 and MDS1-EVI1, both of which are transcription regulators. The inv(3) and t(3;3) abnormalities do not lead to a fusion gene, rather they reposition the distal GATA2 enhancer, resulting in overexpression of EVI1, and simultaneously confer GATA2 haploinsufficiency.[<a href="#cit/section_2.44">

174344</a>

1744,<a href="#cit/section_2.45">

174545</a>

1746] These abnormalities are associated with poor prognosis.[<a href="#cit/section_2.15">

174715</a>

1748,<a href="#cit/section_2.46">

174946</a>

1750,<a href="#cit/section_2.47">

175147</a>

1752] Abnormalities involving MECOM can be detected in some AML cases with other 3q abnormalities and are also associated with poor prognosis.</p>

1753</section>

1754<section id="_470">

1755<h4 id="_470_toc">

1756AML (megakaryoblastic) with t(1;22)(p13.3;q13.3), <em class="gene-name">

1757RBM15-MKL1</em>

1758</h4>

1759<p id="_589" tabindex="-1">

1760The t(1;22)(p13;q13) translocation that produces RBM15-MKL1 is an uncommon driver of pediatric AML (&lt;1% of pediatric AML) and is restricted to acute megakaryocytic leukemia. For more information, see <a href="/types/leukemia/hp/child-aml-treatment-pdq">

1761Childhood Acute Myeloid Leukemia/Other Myeloid Malignancies Treatment</a>

1762.</p>

1763</section>

1764<section id="_471">

1765<h4 id="_471_toc">

1766AML with <em class="gene-name">

1767BCR-ABL1</em>

1768 (provisional entity)</h4>

1769<p id="_590" tabindex="-1">

1770This provisional entity was added by the WHO in 2016 in an effort to recognize that patients with the BCR-ABL1 fusion protein should be treated with a tyrosine kinase inhibitor.[<a href="#cit/section_2.10">

177110</a>

1772] However, this entity is very difficult to distinguish from chronic myelogenous leukemia (CML) in blast phase (BP-CML). Loss of IKZF1 and/or CDKN2A may help distinguish true cases of AML with <em class="gene-name">

1773BCR-ABL1</em>

1774 from BP-CML.[<a href="#cit/section_2.48">

177548</a>

1776] For more information, see <a href="/types/leukemia/hp/cml-treatment-pdq">

1777Chronic Myelogenous Leukemia Treatment</a>

1778.</p>

1779</section>

1780<section id="_472">

1781<h4 id="_472_toc">

1782AML with mutated <em class="gene-name">

1783NPM1</em>

1784</h4>

1785<p id="_591" tabindex="-1">

1786NPM1 is a protein that has been linked to ribosomal protein assembly and transport and is also a molecular chaperone involved in preventing protein aggregation in the nucleolus. Immunohistochemical methods can be used to accurately identify patients with <em class="gene-name">

1787NPM1</em>

1788 mutations by the demonstration of cytoplasmic localization of NPM.[<a href="#cit/section_2.49">

178949</a>

1790] Mutations in the NPM1 protein diminish its nuclear localization and lead to impaired hematopoietic differentiation. They are primarily associated with a normal karyotype (50%), and less commonly seen in conjunction with an abnormal karyotype (&lt;10%), or complex karyotype (&lt;3%).[<a href="#cit/section_2.50">

179150</a>

1792-<a href="#cit/section_2.52">

179352</a>

1794] The presence of an <em class="gene-name">

1795NPM1</em>

1796 mutation confers improved prognosis in the absence of <em class="gene-name">

1797FLT3</em>

1798&#8211;internal tandem duplication (ITD) mutations.[<a href="#cit/section_2.50">

179950</a>

1800,<a href="#cit/section_2.53">

180153</a>

1802,<a href="#cit/section_2.54">

180354</a>

1804] </p>

1805</section>

1806<section id="_473">

1807<h4 id="_473_toc">

1808AML with biallelic mutations of <em class="gene-name">

1809CEBPA</em>

1810</h4>

1811<p id="_592" tabindex="-1">

1812In adults younger than 60 years, 10% to 15% of cytogenetically normal AML cases have mutations in <em class="gene-name">

1813CEBPA</em>

1814.[<a href="#cit/section_2.53">

181553</a>

1816,<a href="#cit/section_2.55">

181755</a>

1818] The <em class="gene-name">

1819CEBPA</em>

1820 gene is located on chromosome 19 and encodes a transcription factor that coordinates myeloid differentiation and cellular growth arrest.[<a href="#cit/section_2.56">

182156</a>

1822] </p>

1823<p id="_593" tabindex="-1">

1824

1825

1826Outcomes for patients with AML with <em class="gene-name">

1827CEBPA</em>

1828 mutations are relatively favorable and similar to that of patients with core-binding factor leukemias.[<a href="#cit/section_2.53">

182953</a>

1830,<a href="#cit/section_2.57">

183157</a>

1832] Studies have demonstrated that <em class="gene-name">

1833CEBPA</em>

1834 double-mutant, but not single-mutant, AML is independently associated with a favorable prognosis,[<a href="#cit/section_2.55">

183555</a>

1836,<a href="#cit/section_2.58">

183758</a>

1838-<a href="#cit/section_2.60">

183960</a>

1840] leading to the WHO 2016 revision that requires biallelic mutations for the disease definition.[<a href="#cit/section_2.10">

184110</a>

1842]

1843</p>

1844</section>

1845<section id="_474">

1846<h4 id="_474_toc">

1847AML with mutated <em class="gene-name">

1848RUNX1</em>

1849 (provisional entity)</h4>

1850<p id="_594" tabindex="-1">

1851AML with mutated <em class="gene-name">

1852RUNX1</em>

1853, which is a provisional entity in the 2016 WHO classification of AML and related neoplasms, denotes a distinct population of de novo AML without myelodysplastic syndrome (MDS)-related features.[<a href="#cit/section_2.61">

185461</a>

1855] Mutations in <em class="gene-name">

1856RUNX1</em>

1857 are associated with a high risk of treatment failure.[<a href="#cit/section_2.62">

185862</a>

1859-<a href="#cit/section_2.64">

186064</a>

1861] </p>

1862</section>

1863</section>

1864<section id="_134">

1865<h3 id="_134_toc">

1866AML With Myelodysplasia-related Features</h3>

1867<p id="_136" tabindex="-1">

1868AML with myelodysplasia-related features is characterized by 20% or more blasts in the blood or bone marrow and dysplasia in two or more myeloid cell lines, generally

1869including megakaryocytes.[<a href="#cit/section_2.5">

18705</a>

1871] To make the diagnosis, dysplasia must be present in 50% or more of the cells of at least two lineages and must be present in a pretreatment bone marrow specimen or must have the presence of an MDS-related cytogenetic abnormality.[<a href="#cit/section_2.5">

18725</a>

1873] AML with myelodysplasia-related features may occur de novo or after MDS or a myelodysplastic/myeloproliferative neoplasm overlap. The diagnostic terminology <em>

1874AML with myelodysplasia-related features evolving from a myelodysplastic syndrome</em>

1875 should be used when an MDS precedes AML.[<a href="#cit/section_2.5">

18765</a>

1877] In the presence of a mutation in <em class="gene-name">

1878NPM1</em>

1879 or biallelic mutations of <em class="gene-name">

1880CEBPA</em>

1881, the presence of multilineage dysplasia alone will not classify a case as AML with myelodysplasia-related changes.[<a href="#cit/section_2.5">

18825</a>

1883] For more information, see <a href="/types/myeloproliferative/hp/myelodysplastic-treatment-pdq">

1884Myelodysplastic Syndromes Treatment</a>

1885 and <a href="/types/myeloproliferative/hp/mds-mpd-treatment-pdq">

1886Myelodysplastic/Myeloproliferative Neoplasms Treatment</a>

1887.</p>

1888<p id="_137" tabindex="-1">

1889AML with myelodysplasia-related features occurs primarily in older patients.[<a href="#cit/section_2.5">

18905</a>

1891] Patients with AML with myelodysplasia-related features frequently present with severe pancytopenia. </p>

1892<p id="_203" tabindex="-1">

1893Common morphologic features include the following: </p>

1894<div class="pdq-content-list">

1895<ul id="_204">

1896<li>

1897Multilineage dysplasia in the blood or bone marrow.</li>

1898<li>

1899Dysplasia in 50% or more of the cells of two or more cell lines.</li>

1900<li>

1901Dysgranulopoiesis (neutrophils with hypogranular cytoplasm, hyposegmented nuclei or bizarrely segmented nuclei).</li>

1902<li>

1903Dyserythropoiesis (megaloblastic nuclei, karyorrhexis, or multinucleation of erythroid precursors and ringed sideroblasts).</li>

1904<li>

1905Dysmegakaryopoiesis (micromegakaryocytes and normal size or large megakaryocytes with monolobed or multiple separated nuclei).</li>

1906</ul>

1907</div>

1908<p id="_138" tabindex="-1">

1909Chromosome abnormalities observed in AML with myelodysplasia-related features are similar to those found in MDS and frequently involve gain or loss of major segments of certain chromosomes, predominately chromosomes 5 and/or 7. The probability of achieving a CR has been reported to be affected adversely by a diagnosis of AML with myelodysplasia-related features.[<a href="#cit/section_2.65">

191065</a>

1911-<a href="#cit/section_2.67">

191267</a>

1913]</p>

1914</section>

1915<section id="_139">

1916<h3 id="_139_toc">

1917Therapy-related Myeloid Neoplasms</h3>

1918<p id="_140" tabindex="-1">

1919Therapy-related myeloid neoplasms (t-MN) include AML (t-AML) and MDS (t-MDS) that arise secondary to cytotoxic chemotherapy and/or radiation therapy.[<a href="#cit/section_2.5">

19205</a>

1921] The therapy-related (or secondary) MDS are included because of their close clinicopathologic relationships to therapy-related AML. Although these therapy-related disorders can be distinguished by the specific mutagenic agents involved, this distinction may be difficult to make because of the frequent overlapping use of multiple potentially mutagenic agents in treating cancer.[<a href="#cit/section_2.68">

192268</a>

1923] Because the associated cytogenetic abnormality, not the mutagenetic agent, determines prognosis and treatment it should be noted in the diagnosis.[<a href="#cit/section_2.10">

192410</a>

1925] </p>

1926<p id="_595" tabindex="-1">

1927Given that t-MN has been associated with germline mutations in cancer susceptibility genes, consideration for germline testing or genetic counseling is warranted in those with strong family histories.[<a href="#cit/section_2.69">

192869</a>

1929] </p>

1930<p id="_141" tabindex="-1">

1931<strong>

1932Alkylating agent-related t-MN</strong>

1933</p>

1934<p id="_142" tabindex="-1">

1935The alkylating agent/radiation-related acute leukemias and myelodysplastic syndromes typically occur 5 to 6 years after exposure to the mutagenic agent, with a reported range of approximately 10 to 192 months.[<a href="#cit/section_2.70">

193670</a>

1937,<a href="#cit/section_2.71">

193871</a>

1939] The risk of occurrence is related to both the total cumulative dose of the alkylating agent and the age of the patient.</p>

1940<p id="_143" tabindex="-1">

1941Cytogenetic abnormalities have been observed in more than 90% of cases of t-MN and commonly include chromosomes 5 and/or 7.[<a href="#cit/section_2.70">

194270</a>

1943,<a href="#cit/section_2.72">

194472</a>

1945,<a href="#cit/section_2.73">

194673</a>

1947] Complex chromosomal abnormalities (&#8805;3 distinct abnormalities) are the most common finding.[<a href="#cit/section_2.68">

194868</a>

1949,<a href="#cit/section_2.72">

195072</a>

1951-<a href="#cit/section_2.74">

195274</a>

1953] </p>

1954<p id="_144" tabindex="-1">

1955<strong>

1956Topoisomerase II inhibitor-related t-MN</strong>

1957</p>

1958<p id="_145" tabindex="-1">

1959Topoisomerase II inhibitor-related t-MN occurs in patients treated with topoisomerase II inhibitors. The agents implicated are the epipodophyllotoxins etoposide and teniposide and the anthracyclines doxorubicin and 4-epi-doxorubicin.[<a href="#cit/section_2.70">

196070</a>

1961] The mean latency period from the time of institution of the causative therapy to the development of t-MN is approximately 2 years.[<a href="#cit/section_2.75">

196275</a>

1963] </p>

1964<p id="_146" tabindex="-1">

1965As with alkylating agent/radiation-related t-MN, the cytogenetic abnormalities are often complex.[<a href="#cit/section_2.68">

196668</a>

1967,<a href="#cit/section_2.72">

196872</a>

1969-<a href="#cit/section_2.74">

197074</a>

1971] The predominant cytogenetic finding involves chromosome 11q23 and the <em class="gene-name">

1972MLL</em>

1973 gene.[<a href="#cit/section_2.68">

197468</a>

1975,<a href="#cit/section_2.76">

197676</a>

1977] </p>

1978</section>

1979<section id="_147">

1980<h3 id="_147_toc">

1981AML, Not Otherwise Specified (NOS)</h3>

1982<p id="_148" tabindex="-1">

1983Cases of AML that do not fulfill the criteria for AML with recurrent genetic abnormalities, AML with myelodysplasia-related features, or t-MN fall within the category of AML, NOS.[<a href="#cit/section_2.10">

198410</a>

1985] As mentioned before, the subcategories of AML, NOS lack prognostic significance when the mutation status of <em class="gene-name">

1986NPM1</em>

1987 and <em class="gene-name">

1988CEBPA</em>

1989 are known.[<a href="#cit/section_2.9">

19909</a>

1991] Classification in this subset of AML is based on leukemic cell features of morphology, cytochemistry, and maturation (i.e., the FAB classification system) and include the following:[<a href="#cit/section_2.5">

19925</a>

1993]</p>

1994<div class="pdq-content-list">

1995<ul id="_596">

1996<li>

1997 AML with minimal differentiation.</li>

1998<li>

1999 AML without maturation.</li>

2000<li>

2001 AML with maturation.</li>

2002<li>

2003 Acute myelomonocytic leukemia.</li>

2004<li>

2005Acute monoblastic/monocytic leukemia.</li>

2006<li>

2007 Pure erythroid leukemia.</li>

2008<li>

2009 Acute megakaryoblastic leukemia.</li>

2010<li>

2011 Acute basophilic leukemia.</li>

2012<li>

2013 Acute panmyelosis with myelofibrosis.</li>

2014</ul>

2015</div>

2016</section>

2017<section id="_485">

2018<h3 id="_485_toc">

2019Myeloid Sarcoma</h3>

2020<p id="_181" tabindex="-1">

2021Myeloid sarcoma (also known as extramedullary myeloid tumor, granulocytic sarcoma, and chloroma) is a tumor mass that consists of myeloblasts or immature myeloid cells, occurring in an extramedullary site.[<a href="#cit/section_2.5">

20225</a>

2023] Development of myeloid sarcoma has been reported in 2% to 8% of patients with AML.[<a href="#cit/section_2.77">

202477</a>

2025] Clinical features include occurrence common in subperiosteal bone structures of the skull, paranasal sinuses, sternum, ribs, vertebrae, and pelvis; lymph nodes, skin, mediastinum, small intestine, and the epidural space; and occurrence de novo or concomitant with AML or a myeloproliferative disorder.[<a href="#cit/section_2.10">

202610</a>

2027,<a href="#cit/section_2.77">

202877</a>

2029,<a href="#cit/section_2.78">

203078</a>

2031]</p>

2032<p id="_242" tabindex="-1">

2033 Morphologic and cytochemical features include the following:</p>

2034<div class="pdq-content-list">

2035<ul id="_243">

2036<li>

2037Granulocytic sarcoma composed of myeloblasts, neutrophils, and neutrophil precursors with three subtypes based on degree of maturation (i.e., blastic, immature, and differentiated).</li>

2038<li>

2039Monoblastic sarcoma preceding or occurring simultaneously with acute monoblastic leukemia.</li>

2040<li>

2041Tumors with trilineage hematopoiesis occurring with transformation of chronic myeloproliferative disorders.</li>

2042<li>

2043Myeloblasts and neutrophils that are positive for MPO.</li>

2044<li>

2045Neutrophils that are positive for naphthol ASD chloroacetate esterase.</li>

2046</ul>

2047</div>

2048<p id="_244" tabindex="-1">

2049Immunophenotyping with antibodies to MPO, lysozyme, and chloroacetate is critical to the diagnosis of these lesions.[<a href="#cit/section_2.5">

20505</a>

2051] The myeloblasts in granulocytic sarcomas express myeloid-associated antigens (CD13, CD33, CD117, and MPO). The monoblasts in monoblastic sarcomas express acute monoblastic leukemia antigens (CD14, CD116, and CD11c) and usually react with antibodies to lysozyme and CD68. The main differential diagnosis includes non-Hodgkin lymphoma of the lymphoblastic type, Burkitt lymphoma, large-cell lymphoma, and small, round-cell tumors, especially in children (e.g., neuroblastoma, rhabdomyosarcoma, Ewing/primitive neuroectodermal tumors, and medulloblastoma). When able, FISH for common chromosomal abnormalities should be completed, as well as molecular studies to refine diagnosis and aid in prognosis.</p>

2052<p id="_182" tabindex="-1">

2053No unique chromosomal abnormalities are associated with myeloid sarcoma.[<a href="#cit/section_2.77">

205477</a>

2055,<a href="#cit/section_2.79">

205679</a>

2057] The presence of myeloid sarcoma in patients with the otherwise good-risk t(8;21) AML may be associated with a lower CR rate and decreased remission duration.[<a href="#cit/section_2.80">

205880</a>

2059] Myeloid sarcoma occurring in the setting of MDS or myeloproliferative disorder is equivalent to blast transformation (progression to AML). In the case of AML, the prognosis is that of the underlying leukemia.[<a href="#cit/section_2.10">

206010</a>

2061] Although the initial presentation of myeloid sarcoma may appear to be isolated, it is a partial manifestation of a systemic disease and should be treated with intensive chemotherapy.[<a href="#cit/section_2.77">

206277</a>

2063,<a href="#cit/section_2.78">

206478</a>

2065,<a href="#cit/section_2.81">

206681</a>

2067,<a href="#cit/section_2.82">

206882</a>

2069]</p>

2070</section>

2071<section id="_486">

2072<h3 id="_486_toc">

2073Myeloid Proliferations Related to Down Syndrome</h3>

2074<p id="_487" tabindex="-1">

2075For more information on TAM and myeloid leukemia associated with Down syndrome, see the <a href="/types/leukemia/hp/child-aml-treatment-pdq#_69">

2076Transient Abnormal Myelopoiesis (TAM) Associated With Down Syndrome</a>

2077 section in Childhood Acute Myeloid Leukemia/Other Myeloid Malignancies Treatment.</p>

2078</section>

2079<section id="_183">

2080<h3 id="_183_toc">

2081Acute Leukemias of Ambiguous Lineage</h3>

2082<p id="_184" tabindex="-1">

2083Acute leukemias of ambiguous lineage are rare types of acute leukemia in which the morphologic, cytochemical, and immunophenotypic features of the blast population do not allow classification in myeloid or lymphoid categories; or the types have morphologic

2084and/or

2085immunophenotypic features of both myeloid and lymphoid cells or both B and T lineages (i.e., acute bilineal leukemia and acute biphenotypic leukemia).[<a href="#cit/section_2.10">

208610</a>

2087,<a href="#cit/section_2.83">

208883</a>

2089,<a href="#cit/section_2.84">

209084</a>

2091] </p>

2092<p id="_597" tabindex="-1">

2093They include the following subcategories:[<a href="#cit/section_2.5">

20945</a>

2095] </p>

2096<div class="pdq-content-list">

2097<ul id="_598">

2098<li>

2099Acute undifferentiated leukemia.</li>

2100<li>

2101Mixed phenotype acute leukemia (MPAL) with t(9;22)(q34.1;q11.2); <em class="gene-name">

2102BCR-ABL1</em>

2103. </li>

2104<li>

2105MPAL with t(v;11q23.3); <em class="gene-name">

2106KMT2A</em>

2107 rearranged. </li>

2108<li>

2109MPAL, B/myeloid, NOS. </li>

2110<li>

2111MPAL, T/myeloid, NOS.</li>

2112</ul>

2113</div>

2114<p id="_599" tabindex="-1">

2115The diagnosis of MPAL is made in leukemias with expression of antigens of more than one lineage:[<a href="#cit/section_2.5">

21165</a>

2117] </p>

2118<table id="_642" class="table-default expandable-container">

2119<caption>

2120Table 1. Mixed Phenotype Acute Leukemia Diagnostic Criteria</caption>

2121<colgroup>

2122<col width="25.83%">

2123<col width="74.16%">

2124</colgroup>

2125<THead>

2126<tr>

2127<th>

2128Diagnosis</th>

2129<th>

2130Criteria</th>

2131</tr>

2132</THead>

2133<TFoot class="pdq-footer">

2134<tr>

2135<td colspan="2">

2136MPO = myeloperoxidase.</td>

2137</tr>

2138</TFoot>

2139<tbody>

2140<tr>

2141<td>

2142Myeloid Lineage</td>

2143<td>

2144MPO (flow cytometry, immunohistochemistry, or cytochemistry) or monocytic differentiation (&#8805; 2 of the following: nonspecific esterase cytochemistry, CD11c, CD14, CD64, lysozyme).</td>

2145</tr>

2146<tr>

2147<td>

2148T-cell Lineage</td>

2149<td>

2150Strong cytoplasmic CD3 (with antibodies to CD3 epsilon chain) or surface CD3.</td>

2151</tr>

2152<tr>

2153<td>

2154B-cell Lineage</td>

2155<td>

2156Strong CD19 with &#8805;1 of the following strongly expressed: cytoplasmic CD79a, cCD22, or CD10; or weak CD19 with at least two of the following strongly expressed: CD79a, cCD22, or CD10.</td>

2157</tr>

2158</tbody>

2159</table>

2160<p id="_185" tabindex="-1">

2161Cytogenetic abnormalities are observed in a high percentage of acute leukemias of ambiguous lineage.[<a href="#cit/section_2.85">

216285</a>

2163-<a href="#cit/section_2.88">

216488</a>

2165] Approximately 33% of cases have the Philadelphia chromosome, and some cases are associated with t(4;11)(q21;q23) or other 11q23 abnormalities. In general, the prognosis appears to be unfavorable. The occurrence of 11q23 abnormalities or <em class="gene-name">

2166BCR-ABL1</em>

2167 are especially unfavorable prognostic indicators;[<a href="#cit/section_2.86">

216886</a>

2169,<a href="#cit/section_2.89">

217089</a>

2171,<a href="#cit/section_2.90">

217290</a>

2173] however, preliminary results indicate that tyrosine kinase inhibitors can be used successfully.[<a href="#cit/section_2.91">

217491</a>

2175,<a href="#cit/section_2.92">

217692</a>

2177] </p>

2178</section>

2577<section id="_660">

2578<h3 id="_660_toc">

2579Phases of Therapy</h3>

2580<p id="_331" tabindex="-1">

2581The treatment of patients with acute myeloid leukemia (AML) is based on whether the disease is newly diagnosed (previously untreated), in remission, or recurrent. Also, the intensity of the treatment and the patient's overall health status are considered when choosing a treatment approach. Successful treatment of AML requires the control of

2582bone marrow and systemic disease, and specific treatment of central nervous

2583system (CNS) disease, if present. The cornerstone of this strategy includes

2584systemically administered combination chemotherapy. Because only 5% or fewer of

2585patients with AML develop CNS disease, prophylactic treatment is not

2586indicated.[<a href="#cit/section_3.1">

25871</a>

2588,<a href="#cit/section_3.2">

25892</a>

2590]</p>

2591<div class="pdq-content-list">

2592<ul id="_657">

2593<li>

2594<strong>

2595Newly diagnosed (untreated):</strong>

2596 Untreated AML is defined as newly diagnosed leukemia that has not been previously treated. The initial treatment for patients with newly diagnosed AML is often induction therapy that aims to induce a remission. In patients with AML, a complete remission (CR) is defined as a normal peripheral blood cell count (absolute neutrophil count &gt;1,000/mm<span class="sup">

25973</span>

2598 and platelet count &gt;100,000/mm<span class="sup">

25993</span>

2600) and normocellular marrow with less than 5% blasts in the marrow and no signs or symptoms of the disease. In addition, no signs or symptoms are evident of CNS leukemia or other extramedullary infiltration.[<a href="#cit/section_3.3">

26013</a>

2602] <p id="_601" tabindex="-1">

2603Modifications to the definition of CR have been proposed because some responses are deeper than a CR, and others may not meet all the criteria for a complete response. In addition, most AML patients meeting the criteria for CR have residual leukemia.[<a href="#cit/section_3.3">

26043</a>

2605] </p>

2606</li>

2607</ul>

2608</div>

2609<table id="_658" class="table-default expandable-container">

2610<caption>

2611Table 2. Treatment Response Categories for Newly Diagnosed Acute Myeloid Leukemia</caption>

2612<colgroup>

2613<col width="50.00%">

2614<col width="50.00%">

2615</colgroup>

2616<THead>

2617<tr>

2618<th>

2619Response Category</th>

2620<th>

2621Definition</th>

2622</tr>

2624<TFoot class="pdq-footer">

2625<tr>

2627ANC = absolute neutrophil count; CR = complete remission; MLFS = morphologic leukemia-free state; PR = partial remission; RT&#8211;qPCR = reverse transcription&#8211;quantitative polymerase chain reaction.</td>

2628</tr>

2629</TFoot>

2630<tbody>

2631<tr>

2632<td>

2633CR without measurable residual disease (CR<sub>

2634MRD&#8722;</sub>

2635)</td>

2636<td>

2637If studied pretreatment, CR with negativity for a genetic marker by RT&#8211;qPCR, or CR with negativity by multicolor flow cytometry.</td>

2638</tr>

2639<tr>

2640<td>

2641CR</td>

2642<td>

2643Bone marrow blasts &lt;5%; absence of circulating blasts and blasts with Auer rods; absence of extramedullary disease; ANC &#8805;1.0 &#215; 10<span class="sup">

26449</span>

2645/L (1,000/microL); platelet count &#8805;100 &#215; 10<span class="sup">

26469</span>

26549</span>

2655/L [1,000/microL]) or thrombocytopenia (&lt;100 &#215; 10<span class="sup">

26569</span>

2657/L [100,000/microL]).</td>

2658</tr>

2659<tr>

2660<td>

2661MLFS</td>

2662<td>

2663Bone marrow blasts &lt;5%; absence of blasts with Auer rods; absence of extramedullary disease; no hematologic recovery required.</td>

2664</tr>

2665<tr>

2666<td>

2667PR</td>

2668<td>

2669All hematologic criteria of CR; decrease of bone marrow blast percentage to 5 to 25%; and decrease of pretreatment bone marrow blast percentage by at least 50%.</td>

2670</tr>

2671</tbody>

2672</table>

2673<div class="pdq-content-list">

2674<ul id="_488">

2675<li>

2676<strong>

2677In remission: </strong>

2678When patients are in a remission after induction chemotherapy, consolidation chemotherapy is given, with the aim of deepening the response and consolidating the remission. This phase of treatment is also called postremission (to maintain remission). Maintenance therapy is not included in most current

2679treatment protocols and clinical trials. Postremission therapy appears to be effective

2680when administered immediately after remission is achieved.[<a href="#cit/section_3.4">

26814</a>

2682]</li>

2683<li>

2684<strong>

2685Persistent/recurrent disease:</strong>

2686 Despite intensive chemotherapy, some patients with newly diagnosed AML will not go into remission and have primary refractory disease. Also, some patients who are in a remission after induction and consolidation chemotherapy may have a return of their disease.[<a href="#cit/section_3.3">

26873</a>

2688] The rates of primary refractory disease and relapse vary with the age of the patient, genomic variants seen in the leukemia cells, and initial treatment given. </li>

2689</ul>

2690</div>

2691<table id="_643" class="table-default expandable-container">

2743</td>

2750</td>

2754 </td>

2756&gt;50% increase in peripheral blasts in the absence of differentiation syndrome; or

2757</td>

2758</tr>

2768<section id="_493">

2769<h3 id="_493_toc">

2770Supportive Care During Therapy</h3>

2771<p id="_50" tabindex="-1">

2772Because myelosuppression is an anticipated consequence of both the leukemia and

2773its treatment with chemotherapy, patients must be closely monitored during

2774therapy. Facilities must be available for hematologic support with multiple

2775blood fractions, including platelet transfusions, and for the treatment

2776of related infectious complications.[<a href="#cit/section_3.5">

27775</a>

2778] </p>

2779<section id="_571">

2780<h4 id="_571_toc">

2781Transfusion therapy</h4>

2782<p id="_495" tabindex="-1">

2783Supportive care during remission induction treatment should routinely include

2784red blood cell and platelet transfusions when appropriate.[<a href="#cit/section_3.6">

27856</a>

2786,<a href="#cit/section_3.7">

27877</a>

2788] Rapid marrow ablation

2789with consequent earlier marrow regeneration decreases morbidity and mortality.

2790Randomized trials have shown similar

2791outcomes for patients who received prophylactic platelet transfusions at a

2792level of 10,000/mm<span class="sup">

27933</span>

2794 rather than 20,000/mm<span class="sup">

27953</span>

2796.[<a href="#cit/section_3.8">

27978</a>

2798] The incidence of platelet alloimmunization was similar among

2799groups randomly assigned to receive pooled platelet concentrates from random

2800donors; filtered, pooled platelet concentrates from random donors; ultraviolet

2801B-irradiated, pooled platelet concentrates from random donors; or filtered

2802platelets obtained by apheresis from single random donors.[<a href="#cit/section_3.9">

28039</a>

2804]</p>

2805<p id="_602" tabindex="-1">

2806No good evidence exists to support granulocyte transfusions in the treatment of AML. A multicenter randomized trial (<a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT00627393">

2807RING</a>

2808 [NCT00627393]) to address the utility of granulocyte transfusions in the setting of infections was conducted.[<a href="#cit/section_3.10">

280910</a>

2810] There was no difference between the granulocyte and control arms for the composite primary end point of survival plus microbial response at 42 days after randomization. However, the power to detect a true beneficial effect was low because enrollment was half that of the planned study size.</p>

2811</section>

2812<section id="_572">

2813<h4 id="_572_toc">

2814Growth factors</h4>

2815<p id="_644" tabindex="-1">

2816The following growth factors have been studied in the treatment of AML:</p>

2817<div class="pdq-content-list">

2818<ul id="_603">

2819<li>

2820<strong>

2821Colony&#8211;stimulating factors:</strong>

2822 Granulocyte colony&#8211;stimulating factor and granulocyte-macrophage colony&#8211;stimulating factor have been

2823studied in an effort to shorten the period of granulocytopenia associated with

2824leukemia treatment.[<a href="#cit/section_3.11">

282511</a>

2826] If used, these agents are administered after administration

2827of chemotherapy. Although the use of growth factors decreases the time to neutrophil recovery by 2 to 5 days, and decreases rates of febrile neutropenia and duration of hospitalization, randomized clinical trials have not shown an impact of growth factors on overall survival and their cost-effectiveness has been inconsistently reported.[<a href="#cit/section_3.12">

282812</a>

2829,<a href="#cit/section_3.13">

283013</a>

2831] Use of growth factors is not routinely recommended in the remission induction setting.</li>

2832<li>

2833<strong>

2834 Erythropoiesis-stimulating agents:</strong>

2835 Anemia associated with the diagnosis of AML and induction chemotherapy is managed primarily with red blood transfusions. Unlike myelodysplastic syndromes, there is no role for the use of erythropoiesis stimulating agents (e.g., epoetin alfa and darbepoetin) during the treatment of AML.</li>

2836<li>

2837<strong>

2838Thrombopoietin mimetics:</strong>

2839 Clinical trials have assessed the use of thrombopoietin mimetics in the treatment of AML. Eltrombopag as a single agent was tested in a multicenter, randomized, placebo-controlled, double-blind, phase I/II trial of 98 patients with platelet counts less than 30x10<span class="sup">

28409</span>

2841/L as a result of AML or MDS. No significant improvements in platelet counts were recorded. Significant hemorrhage was reported in ten (16%) patients given eltrombopag and nine (26%) patients given placebo. No difference in disease progression or overall survival was observed.[<a href="#cit/section_3.14">

284214</a>

2843]<p id="_604" tabindex="-1">

2844Eltrombopag appeared to hasten platelet recovery and reduce the number of platelet transfusions needed when added in an unblinded fashion to induction chemotherapy in older <em class="gene-name">

2845FLT3</em>

2846-negative AML patients.[<a href="#cit/section_3.15">

284715</a>

2848] However, in a separate, randomized double-blind study of 148 patients, eltrombopag or placebo was added to high-dose induction chemotherapy.[<a href="#cit/section_3.16">

284916</a>

2850] The results of this study did not indicate any clinical benefit of eltrombopag over placebo. Given the minimal efficacy signal at this point, eltrombopag is not routinely recommended in the supportive care or remission induction setting.</p>

2851</li>

2852</ul>

2853</div>

2854</section>

2855<section id="_573">

2856<h4 id="_573_toc">

2857Antimicrobial therapy</h4>

2858<p id="_58" tabindex="-1">

2859Empiric

2860broad spectrum antimicrobial therapy is an absolute necessity for febrile

2861patients who are profoundly neutropenic.[<a href="#cit/section_3.17">

286217</a>

2863,<a href="#cit/section_3.18">

286418</a>

2865] Careful instruction in

2866personal hand hygiene, dental care, and recognition of early signs of infection are

2867appropriate in all patients. Elaborate isolation facilities (including

2868filtered air, sterile food, and gut flora sterilization) are not indicated.[<a href="#cit/section_3.19">

286919</a>

2870,<a href="#cit/section_3.20">

287120</a>

2872] Likewise, there are no advantages to eating a cooked neutropenic diet, as demonstrated in randomized trials.[<a href="#cit/section_3.21">

287321</a>

2874] </p>

2875<p id="_605" tabindex="-1">

2876Antibiotic prophylaxis with a fluoroquinolone and antifungal prophylaxis with an oral triazole or parenteral echinocandin is appropriate for patients with expected prolonged, profound neutropenia (&lt;100/mm<span class="sup">

28773</span>

2878 for 2 weeks for profound neutropenia lasting &gt;7 days).[<a href="#cit/section_3.22">

287922</a>

2880] Unlike patients undergoing treatment for acute lymphoblastic lymphoma, <em>

2881Pneumocystis jirovecii</em>

2882 prophylaxis is not routinely employed.</p>

2883<p id="_606" tabindex="-1">

2884Nucleoside analog-based antiviral prophylaxis, such as acyclovir, is appropriate for patients who are seropositive for herpes simplex virus undergoing induction chemotherapy.[<a href="#cit/section_3.22">

288522</a>

2886]</p>

2887</section>

2888</section>
